And for the two eyes is the full diyah, and for the two hands is the full diyah, and for the two feet is the full diyah, and for the two ears is the full diyah, and for the two lips is the full diyah, and for the two testicles is the full diyah, and for the two breasts of a woman is the full diyah. And for each single one of these things is half the diyah. Explanation: For both eyes, full diyah; for both hands, full diyah; for both feet, full diyah; for both ears, full diyah; for both lips, full diyah; for both testicles, full diyah; and for both breasts of a woman, full diyah. If these organs are both lost, the benefit of the person is lost, so full diyah is obligatory. And if one organ is lost, like one eye, then half diyah is obligatory. The hadith of Amr ibn Hazm has the details. The portion of the hadith is: 'The Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) wrote to the people of Yemen a book containing the obligations, the sunan, and the diyat... and for the two lips is the diyah, and for the two testicles is the diyah, and for the penis is the diyah, and for the spine is the diyah, and for the two eyes is the diyah, and for one foot is half the diyah.' (Nasa'i, etc.) And from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab: 'He said regarding one breast of a woman: half the diyah, and for both of them is the diyah.' This athar and hadith clarify the diyat for all organs. [Explanation of terms]. [Ruling 10] Half diyah for one of these things. Example: Full diyah for both eyes is one hundred camels, so diyah for one eye is half, i.e., fifty camels. Same for all paired organs: full diyah for losing both, half diyah for losing one. In the hadith of Amr ibn Hazm: 'But he said: And for one eye is half the diyah, and for one hand is half the diyah, and for one foot is half the diyah.' (Marginal notes).
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage states the ruling for diyah (blood money) on paired organs. The text enumerates specific pairs: eyes, hands, feet, ears, lips, testicles, and a woman's breasts. Each pair, when both are lost, incurs full diyah (100 camels). If only one of the pair is lost, half diyah (50 camels) is due. The commentator explains that the rationale is that losing both removes the entire benefit of that organ for the person. The proof is from the famous hadith of 'Amr ibn Hazm, which is a foundational text for diyat rulings in the Hanafi school. Additionally, an athar from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab confirms the same for a woman's breasts. The commentator clarifies the Arabic terms: 'shafatan' is dual of 'shafah' (lips), 'unthiyan' is dual of 'unthyah' (testicle), 'thady' is breast. The ruling is clear: for each single loss, exactly half of the full diyah for that pair applies. This is consistent across all listed organs, as the principle is based on the loss of paired benefit.

Key Terms
دية (diyah): Blood money; financial compensation paid to the victim or heirs for bodily harm or homicide.
شفتان (shafataan): The two lips; a paired organ.
أنثيان (unthiyaan): The two testicles; a paired organ.
ثدي (thady): Breast; specifically of a woman in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Paired organs incur full diyah when both are lost, and half diyah when one is lost, as established by the hadith of 'Amr ibn Hazm.

Diyah for Eyelashes: An Analysis of the Hanafi Ruling
Ruling on diyah for eyelashes with proof from companion statement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi diyah amounts for eyelashes (full diyah for all four eyelids, quarter diyah for one) and explain the proof from the athar of Zayd ibn Thabit.

Core Idea
Diyah for eyelashes is calculated per eyelid: full diyah for all four eyelids, quarter diyah for one.

This lesson addresses the diyah (financial compensation) for permanent destruction of eyelashes. The Hanafi school, relying on an athar from the Companion Zayd ibn Thabit, specifies precise amounts. Understanding this ruling illustrates the school's use of companion statements (qawl sahabi) as a source of law in the absence of direct Qur'anic or prophetic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the two key rulings: (1) destruction of all eyelashes on both eyes (all four eyelids) requires full diyah (100 camels); (2) destruction of eyelashes on a single eyelid requires quarter diyah (25 camels).
This is based on counting each eyelid as a separate organ.
2
Examine the Proof
The proof is the athar from Zayd ibn Thabit: 'For the eyelid of the eye, quarter of the diyah.' This is a qawl sahabi, a statement of a companion, which is a valid source in Hanafi usul.
Athar is transmitted by Bayhaqi, Abd al-Razzaq, and Ibn Abi Shayba.
3
Understand the Wajh al-Dalalah
The athar explicitly gives quarter diyah for one eyelid (jafn). Since there are four eyelids (upper and lower of each eye), the total for all four is four quarters, i.e., full diyah.
The Hanafi school extends the specific ruling to the implied full by multiplication.
4
Note the Authority and Intra-Madhhab Status
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah, the core authoritative transmission of the school. There is no known intra-Hanafi disagreement (Sahibayn agree).
Some other schools may not consider eyelashes as a separate basis for diyah, but the Hanafi position is relied upon.
5
Apply to a Scenario
If a person intentionally plucks out all eyelashes from one eyelid, the diyah is 25 camels. If all four eyelids are affected, it is 100 camels.
The same rule applies if the loss is due to an accident; diyah is wajib.

Source Text

وفى اشفار العينين الدية وفى احدهما ربع الدية. دونوں آنکھوں کی پلکوں میں پوری دیت ہے اور ان میں سے ایک میں چوتھائی دیت ہے۔ ہر آنکھ میں دو پلکیں ہوتی ہیں تو دونوں آنکھوں میں چار پلکیں ہوئیں۔ اگر تمام پلکوں کے بال اکھیڑ دیئے جائیں تو پوری دیت سو اونٹ لازم ہوں گے۔ اثر میں ہے۔ عن زيد بن ثابت في جفن العين ربع الدية (الف) سن للبیہقی ، باب دیتہ اشفار العینین ج عامن ص ۸۷ نمبر ۱۶۲۳۶ / مصنف عبد الرزاق ، باب حفر العین ج تاسع ، ص ۳۲۲ نمبر ۱۷۳۸۵ار مصنف ابن ابی هیسته ۲۰ الاشفار ما قالوا فيها ؟ ج خامس بص ۳۵۸ نمبر ۲۶۸۷۲) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ چاروں پلکوں میں ایک دیت اور ایک پلک میں چوتھائی دیت لازم ہوگی۔ اشفار : شفر کی جمع ہے۔ پلک کی جڑ یہاں پلک کے بال مراد ہیں۔
And for the eyelashes of the two eyes is the full diyah, and for one of them is a quarter of the diyah. The explanation: For the eyelashes of both eyes, full diyah is due, and for one of them, quarter diyah. Each eye has two eyelids, so both eyes have four eyelids. If all the eyelashes are plucked out, full diyah of one hundred camels is obligatory. There is an athar from Zayd ibn Thabit: 'For the eyelid of the eye, quarter of the diyah.' (Bayhaqi, Abd al-Razzaq, Ibn Abi Shayba) From this athar, it is understood that for all four eyelids, full diyah is due, and for one eyelid, quarter diyah. 'Ashfar' is the plural of 'shifr', meaning the root of the eyelid, here meaning the eyelashes.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage addresses the diyah for eyelashes. The ruling distinguishes between the two eyes' lashes collectively and each individual eyelid's lashes. Since each eye has two eyelids (upper and lower), total four eyelids. If all lashes of all four eyelids are destroyed, full diyah (100 camels) is wajib. If only one eyelid's lashes are destroyed, one-fourth of the full diyah (25 camels) is due. The proof is the athar from Zayd ibn Thabit, a companion, who explicitly stated 'for the eyelid of the eye, quarter of the diyah'. This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school. The commentator clarifies that 'ashfar' technically refers to the root of the eyelid, but in this context means the eyelashes themselves.

Key Terms
أشفار (ashfar): Plural of shifr; roots of eyelashes, here meaning the eyelashes themselves.
جفن (jafn): Eyelid; each eye has an upper and lower eyelid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) No diyah for a single eyelid

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, diyah for eyelashes is calculated per eyelid: quarter diyah per eyelid, full diyah for all four eyelids, based on the companion statement of Zayd ibn Thabit.

Diyah for Fingers: Equal Compensation for All Digits
Ruling on diyah for each finger with hadith proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the diyah for each finger according to the Hanafi school and explain the prophetic proof for its equal amount.

Core Idea
Each finger of hand or foot carries exactly one-tenth of full diyah—10 camels—with no distinction between thumb and little finger.

This passage from the Hanafi diyat manual presents the clear ruling for compensation for fingers and toes. While common intuition might suggest that larger or more functional digits deserve higher blood money, the Prophet ﷺ explicitly declared all digits equal. In this lesson, we will unpack the ruling, examine its textual evidence, and understand the legal reasoning behind this uniformity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hukm and its subject
The passage rules on the diyah for each finger of hands and feet. Determine the hukm level (wajib) and the specific amount (10 camels).
Diyah is wajib upon the 'aqila for unintentional injuries.
2
Examine the proof (dalil)
The hadith of Ibn Abbas states: 'The diyah of the fingers of the hands and feet is equal: ten camels for each finger.' Identify the type of proof (Sunnah ahad) and its strengths.
The hadith is a clear textual proof, but its chain is ahad (single narrator).
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah (legal import)
The proof explicitly mentions 'equal' and specifies 'ten camels for each finger'. This eliminates any gradation based on finger size or function. The companion hadith 'These and these are equal' reinforces that even the little finger and thumb are the same.
The text also extends this to toes by qiyas.
4
Check for madhhab differences
According to the source, this ruling is muttafaq alayh (agreed upon) among schools. The Hanafi position is strong and unanimous, with no intra-madhhab variation.
This uniformity simplifies practice in fatwa and qada'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي كل اصبع من اصابع اليدين والرجلين عشر الدية والاصابع كلها سواء. ہاتھ اور پیر کی ہر انگلی کی دیت دسواں حصہ ہے اور تمام انگلیاں برابر ہیں۔ دو ہاتھوں میں دس انگلیاں ہوتی ہیں تو ایک انگلی کی دیت پوری دیت یعنی سو اونٹ کا دسواں حصہ ہوگا۔ یعنی ایک انگلی کی دبیت دس اونٹ ہوں گے۔ اور اسی طرح پاؤں کی ہر انگلی کی دیت دس اونٹ ہوں گے۔ اور تمام انگلیوں کا درجہ دیت میں برابر ہے۔ حدیث میں ہے۔ عن ابن عباس قال قال رسول الله الا الله دية اصابع اليدين والرجلين سواء عشرة من الابل لكل اصبع ۔ اور اگلی حدیث میں ہے۔ عن ابن عباس عن النبي الا الله قال هذه وهذه سواء يعنى الخنصر والابهام (ب)(ترندی شریف، باب ماجاء فی دیتہ الاصابع نمبر ۱۳۹۲/۱۳۹۱ رنسائی شریف، ذکر حدیث عمرو بن حزم فی العقول ص ۶۶۹ نمبر ۴۸۶۰ ) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ ہر انگلی کی دیت دس اونٹ ہے۔ اور تمام انگلیوں کا درجہ برابر ہے۔ پیر کی انگلیوں کا بھی یہی حال ہے۔
And for each finger of the fingers of the hands and feet is one-tenth of the diyah, and all fingers are equal. The diyah of each finger of hand and foot is one-tenth, and all fingers are equal. The two hands have ten fingers, so the diyah of one finger is one-tenth of the full diyah, i.e., one hundred camels, so one finger's diyah is ten camels. Similarly, each toe of the foot has a diyah of ten camels. All fingers are equal in the degree of diyah. In the hadith: From Ibn Abbas, he said: The Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) said: 'Indeed, the diyah of the fingers of the hands and feet is equal: ten camels for each finger.' And in another hadith: From Ibn Abbas from the Prophet (ﷺ): 'These and these are equal,' meaning the little finger and the thumb. (Tirmidhi, Nasa'i). From this hadith it is known that each finger's diyah is ten camels, and all fingers are equal. The same applies to toes.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage establishes the diyah for fingers. Each finger of either the hand or the foot incurs one-tenth of the full diyah, i.e., 10 camels. Importantly, all fingers are equal in this amount, whether the thumb or the little finger, and whether on the hand or the foot. The proof is the hadith of Ibn Abbas where the Prophet explicitly stated that all fingers are equal, each carrying ten camels. This ruling is well-established in the Hanafi school. The commentator notes that the same applies to toes, based on qiyas (analogy) to the hadith. This equality is a point of distinction; some other schools may differentiate between fingers, but the Hanafi position is uniform.

Key Terms
إصبع (isba'): Finger or toe; includes all digits of hands and feet.
عشر الدية ('ushr al-diyah): One-tenth of the full diyah; for standard 100 camels, this is 10 camels.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Diyah for Finger Joints According to Number of Joints
Ruling on diyah for individual finger joints based on number of joints

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyah for a single finger joint and calculate the amount due based on the number of joints in that finger.

Core Idea
For a finger joint, diyah is proportional: one-third if the finger has three joints, half if it has two.

In Hanafi law, the diyah (blood money) for a whole finger is fixed at ten camels. But what if only a single joint is lost? This lesson presents the proportional rule: the diyah for one joint depends on the total number of joints in that finger. For fingers with three joints (most fingers), one joint carries one-third of the finger's diyah; for the thumb or big toe with two joints, one joint carries half. This precise rule ensures just compensation for partial loss.

Guided Path
1
Identify the finger and its joint count
First, determine whether the injured finger has three joints (e.g., index, middle, ring, little) or two joints (thumb or big toe).
2
Recall the diyah for one whole finger
In Hanafi law, the complete diyah for a finger is 10 camels (or equivalent value). Store this baseline.
Diyah rates are standardised in classical Hanafi texts.
3
Apply the proportional fraction
If the finger has three joints, each joint gets 1/3 of 10 camels = 3.33 camels. If it has two joints, each joint gets 1/2 of 10 camels = 5 camels.
The fraction matches the number of joints, not the position of the joint.
4
State the ruling concisely
The ruling is: 'For every finger that has three joints, for one joint one-third of the diyah of the finger; and for what has two joints, for one joint half the diyah of the finger.'
This is a memorisation anchor (matn).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفی كل اصبع فيها ثلثة مفاصل ففى احدهما ثلث دية الاصابع وما فيها مفصلان ففى احدهما نصف دية الاصابع. ہر وہ انگلی جس میں تین گرہیں ہیں تو ایک گرہ کٹ جائے تو ایک انگلی کی تہائی دیت ہے اور جس میں دوگر ہیں ہیں تو اس کے ایک میں انگلی کی آدھی دیت ہے۔ تشریح جن جن انگلیوں میں تین تین گرہیں ہیں اگر ان میں سے ایک گرہ کٹ جائے تو ایک انگلی کی جو دس اونٹ دیت ہے اس کی تہائی دیت یعنی تین اونٹ اور ایک اونٹ کی تہائی 3.33 اونٹ لاز ہوں گے ۔ کیونکہ ایک انگلی کی دیت دس اونٹ ہیں تو تین جوڑ میں سے ایک جوڑ کی دیت
And for every finger that has three joints, for one of them is one-third of the diyah of the finger; and what has two joints, for one of them is half the diyah of the finger. Every finger that has three knuckles, if one knuckle is cut, then one-third of the diyah of that finger is due; and for a finger that has two knuckles, for one of them is half the diyah of that finger. Explanation: For those fingers that have three knuckles each, if one knuckle is cut, then from the diyah of one finger which is ten camels, one-third is due, i.e., three and one-third camels (3.33). Because the diyah of one finger is ten camels, so from three joints, the diyah of one joint is...
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage details the diyah for individual finger joints (sing. mifsal, pl. mafasil). The rule is proportional to the number of joints in that finger. The standard diyah for a whole finger is 10 camels. Fingers on the hand typically have three joints (knuckles) except the thumb which has two joints. For a three-joint finger, each joint carries one-third of 10 camels, i.e., 3 and 1/3 camels. For a two-joint finger (thumb or big toe), each joint carries half, i.e., 5 camels. The commentator illustrates with the example: one-third of 10 camels is approximately 3.33 camels. This proportional method ensures that the diyah is distributed according to the number of segments. The text here is purely the ruling without proof, serving as a memorization anchor.

Key Terms
مفصل (mifsal): Joint (knuckle) of a finger; the segment between two phalanges.
ثلث دية الاصابع (thulth diyat al-asabi'): One-third of the diyah of the finger (about 3.33 camels).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) One-third of the finger's diyah

D) The full finger's diyah

Key Takeaway
The diyah for a finger joint is proportional to the number of joints in that finger: one-third for three-joint fingers, half for two-joint fingers.

Diyah for Eyelids (Ashfar al-'Aynayn)
Ruling on diyah for eyelids and their proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyah for eyelids and explain the proof from Zayd ibn Thabit.

Core Idea
Full diyah for four eyelids, quarter for one — based on a companion's ruling.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling regarding diyah (blood money) for the eyelashes (ashfar) of both eyes. The matn establishes that if all four eyelash sets are completely removed, the full diyah of one hundred camels is due, and for a single set, one quarter. The proof is an athar from Zayd ibn Thabit, providing a clear precedent from the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What injury triggers diyah? The complete removal of the eyelash hair (ashfar) of the eyelids. The text states that for all four eyelids (upper and lower of both eyes), the full diyah is due, and for one eyelid, a quarter diyah.
Each eye has two eyelids, total four.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is an athar from Zayd ibn Thabit (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, etc.): 'In the eyelid of the eye is quarter diyah.' This explicit statement establishes the amount for a single eyelid.
The athar is considered zanni al-thubut but sufficient for establishing the ruling.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The athar explicitly states quarter diyah for one eyelid. By logical extension, four eyelids (all ashfar of both eyes) result in full diyah (4 × 1/4 = 1). This demonstrates the legal link between the proof and the ruling.
The athar serves as a specific precedent; no need for qiyas.
4
Note the scope and definition
The term 'ashfar' (plural of shifr) specifically means the eyelash hair, not the eyelid itself. Therefore, the ruling applies only to removal of the hair, not the eyelid skin.
This distinction is crucial to avoid misapplication.

The Diyah of Fingers: Equality of All Digits
Ruling with hadith proof on finger diyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the diyah for each finger and explain the Hanafi principle of equality among all fingers of hands and feet.

Core Idea
Every finger, whether thumb or little finger, carries an equal diyah of ten camels.

This lesson deals with the fixed compensation (diyah) for the loss of fingers. The Hanafi school rules that all fingers — hands and feet — are equal in diyah, with each finger carrying one-tenth of the full diyah. We will examine the textual evidence from hadith and the reasoning behind this uniformity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the passage carefully. What is the diyah for each finger? What is the full diyah?
Full diyah is 100 camels; each finger is 10 camels.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites two hadith from Ibn Abbas. Identify the key phrase that establishes the amount and equality.
The Prophet said: 'The diyah of fingers of hands and feet is equal: ten camels for each finger.'
3
Understand the equality principle
The second hadith explicitly equates the little finger (khinsar) and the thumb (ibham). Why does the Hanafi school emphasize this?
This removes any doubt about size or function affecting diyah.
4
Note cross-madhhab difference
Some schools differentiate between fingers based on utility. The Hanafi position relies on the literal wording of the hadith.
The practical consequence is uniformity in compensation.
5
Apply to feet
The text extends the same ruling to toes. Confirm that the same principle applies.
Hands and feet are treated identically.

The two hands have ten fingers, so diyah for one finger is one-tenth of the full diyah, i.e., ten camels. Similarly, each toe has ten camels. All fingers are equal in diyah. Hadith: Ibn Abbas reported that the Prophet said: 'The diyah of fingers of hands and feet is equal: ten camels for each finger.' Another hadith: 'This and this are equal' meaning the little finger and the thumb.]
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling for diyah on fingers: each finger of hand or foot carries one-tenth of the full diyah, i.e., ten camels. All fingers are equal, including the thumb and little finger. The author provides two hadith proofs: one general statement from Ibn Abbas that all fingers are equal at ten camels each, and another specifically stating that the thumb and little finger are equal. This equality applies to both hands and feet. The Hanafi position is clear: no distinction between fingers.

Key Terms
اصابع (asabi'): Fingers of hands and feet.
خنصر (khinsar): The little finger.
ابهام (ibham): The thumb.

Each finger, regardless of hand or foot, incurs a fixed diyah of one-tenth of the full diyah, i.e., ten camels, based on explicit prophetic statement.

Diyah for Finger Joints
Ruling on diyah for finger joints

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the diyah for a single finger joint according to the Hanafi school and identify the athar evidence that supports this proportional ruling.

Core Idea
A finger joint's diyah is proportional to the number of joints: one-third for three-joint fingers, half for the thumb's two-joint structure.

In Hanafi fiqh, the diyah (blood money) for a whole finger is fixed at ten camels. But what if only one joint is lost? This lesson examines the proportional rule: each joint carries a fraction of the finger's diyah based on the total number of joints. We also explore the athar (reports) from the Companions that support this division.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the loss of a finger joint due to injury. The ruling (hukm) is that wajib compensation (diyah) is due. What is the precise amount?
Diyah is a fixed financial compensation for bodily harm.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites athar (reports) from Umar ibn al-Khattab and Ibrahim al-Nakha'i. These are the evidence for the proportional division.
The dalil type is athar, which is a report from a Companion or early authority, considered zanni in transmission.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit text 'fi kulli anmalatin thulthu diyati al-isba'' (every knuckle has one-third of finger diyah) directly gives the rule. For the thumb, Ibrahim's report specifies half per joint due to only two joints.
The proof is direct and clear (zahir al-dalalah).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Hanafi position is supported by these athar. Other schools may follow different traditions or analogies, but the Hanafi school relies on this proportional method.
Hanafi position is mufta bihi and from Zahir al-Riwayah.
5
Determine the proportional calculation
For a three-joint finger: one joint diyah = 1/3 of 10 camels ≈ 3.33 camels. For thumb (two joints): one joint = 1/2 of 10 = 5 camels. The same proportion applies to monetary equivalents (gold/silver).
The calculation is straightforward once the ratio is known.

[Urdu: Every finger that has three joints: if one joint is cut, one-third of the finger's diyah is due. For the thumb, which has two joints, one joint gives half the finger's diyah. Explanation: For three-joint fingers, one joint diyah = 10 camels / 3 = 3.33 camels or 33.33 dinars or 333.33 dirhams. For two-joint thumb, one joint = 5 camels or 50 dinars or 500 dirhams. Athar from Umar: every knuckle (anmalah) has one-third finger diyah. From Ibrahim: every joint has one-third except thumb which has two joints each half.]

Commentary
This passage explains the diyah for individual finger joints. Each finger normally has three joints (except thumb which has two). For a three-joint finger, cutting off one joint incurs one-third of the finger's diyah (approx 3.33 camels). For the thumb, one joint incurs half of its diyah (5 camels). The author cites athar from Umar ibn al-Khattab and Ibrahim al-Nakha'i. The footnote clarifies that 'anmalah' means knuckle. This is consistent with the Hanafi position that each joint's diyah is proportional to the number of joints.

Key Terms
مفصل (mufassal): Joint of a finger; the knuckle.
انملة (anmalah): Fingertip or knuckle; here used for joint.

Key Takeaway
The diyah for a finger joint is proportional to the number of joints: one-third for three-joint fingers, half for the thumb's two joints, as supported by athar from Umar and Ibrahim.

Diyah for All Teeth: Equality in the Hanafi School
Ruling on diyah for teeth and molars with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the diyah for each tooth according to the Hanafi school and explain the proof from the hadith of Amr ibn Hazm and Ibn Abbas.

Core Idea
All teeth, including molars, carry equal diyah of five camels each.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on diyah (blood money) for teeth. The text establishes that each tooth, regardless of type, incurs a fixed penalty of five camels. The author supports this with two ahadith that explicitly state the equality of all teeth and fingers. Understanding this ruling clarifies a common area of dispute between madhahib and provides a clear example of reliance on explicit textual evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is loss of a tooth (or molar). The ruling is that the perpetrator must pay diyah: five camels per tooth.
Diyah is wajib, a fixed compensation.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites two hadith: the letter of Amr ibn Hazm (specifying five camels per tooth) and the hadith of Ibn Abbas (stating all teeth are equal).
Both are ahad hadith (zanni al-thubut) but accepted for establishing rulings in the Hanafi school.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit wording 'al-thaniyah wa al-dirs sawa' (the front tooth and the molar are equal) directly proves that molars have the same diyah as incisors.
No need for qiyas; the text is clear.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools (e.g., certain Shafi'i views) differentiate between teeth based on function or size. The Hanafi school rejects differentiation.
The dispute is based on interpretation of the hadith 'al-asnan sawa'.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi and relies on the explicit text. The hadith is unambiguous, so there is no room for distinction.
This ruling is part of Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text

The author provides two hadith proofs: the famous letter of Amr ibn Hazm which specifies five camels per tooth, and a hadith from Ibn Abbas stating equality of all teeth. The footnote clarifies that 'thinayah' means front tooth and 'dirs' means molar. The Hanafi position is clear: no distinction between teeth.

Full Diyah for Loss of Limb Function
Ruling on full diyah for limb that loses function

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyah when a limb loses its function and explain the analogical reasoning (qiyas) that equates it to amputation.

Core Idea
Loss of a limb's function triggers the same full diyah as cutting it off — the benefit is the real value.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary on diyat establishes a critical principle: the law does not merely look at physical severance but at the loss of the limb's beneficial function. Using a clear qiyas, the author equates complete paralysis or loss of sight to amputation. In this lesson, we will unpack the ruling, the analogy, and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is striking a limb causing complete loss of its function. The ruling is that full diyah for that limb is due.
The passage explicitly states 'full diyah' for the limb, not for a person.
2
Understand the analogical reasoning (qiyas)
The asl (original case) is cutting off a limb, which incurs full diyah by Sunnah and consensus. The far' (new case) is causing loss of function without severance. The illah (effective cause) is complete loss of benefit (manfa'ah). Since the illah is present in both, the same hukm applies.
The illah is 'jaliyyah' (clear) because the harm is equally severe.
3
Apply to concrete examples
For a paralyzed hand, full diyah of the hand (50 camels) is due. For loss of sight, full diyah of the eye (50 camels) is due. The Urdu commentary provides these examples.
The diyah of a limb is calculated as a fraction of full diyah (100 camels for a person).
4
Note intra-madhhab consensus
Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani all agree on this ruling. There is no known Hanafi disagreement.
5
Contrast with potential cross-madhhab difference
Some schools may require an assessment of the degree of disability to determine proportionality, rather than treating complete loss as equal to severance.
This difference is legitimate and based on different usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ضرب عضوا فاذهب منفعته ففيه دية كاملة كما لو قطعه

And whoever strikes a limb and causes its benefit to cease, then in it is full diyah as if he cut it off. [Urdu: If someone strikes a limb and its benefit is lost, full diyah is due, just as if it were cut off. Example: hand becomes paralyzed, eye loses sight. The hand remains but is useless; it is considered as if cut off. So full diyah of hand (50 camels) or eye is due.]
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage establishes that causing a limb to lose its function (like paralysis of the hand or loss of sight) incurs the same full diyah as physically cutting off the limb. The reasoning is analogical (qiyas): the harm is equivalent. The author gives examples: a paralyzed hand gets 50 camels (half of full diyah since hand is half of body? Actually full diyah for a limb is according to its value; for a hand it's half of full diyah? Wait, the text says 'full diyah' and then says 'pachas oont' which is 50 camels, half of 100. But the ruling is 'diyah kamilah' which may mean the full diyah of that limb, not necessarily full diyah of a person. The Urdu example clarifies: for a hand, the diyah is 50 camels (half of full diyah). The key point: loss of function is treated like loss of the limb itself.

Key Terms
منفعة (manfa'ah): The beneficial function of a limb (e.g., sight, grip).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Diyat — Blood Money

Diyah for Finger Joints and Teeth
States diyah amounts for finger joints and teeth with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the diyah amounts for finger joints and teeth according to the Hanafi school, and identify the proof texts that establish these amounts.

Core Idea
Each tooth is worth five camels; each finger joint is one-third of the finger's diyah, except thumb joints are half.

In Hanafi fiqh, diyah (blood money) is fixed for specific bodily injuries. This lesson examines the precise amounts for injuries to finger joints and teeth, based on prophetic hadith and the practice of the Companions. Understanding these rulings is essential for applying Islamic criminal law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on finger joints
Note that for each finger except the thumb, there are three joints. The diyah for a full finger is ten camels. Therefore, each joint (excluding thumb) carries one-third of that, i.e., 3.33 camels. For the thumb, which has two joints, each joint carries half the thumb's diyah (i.e., 5 camels).
The thumb's exception is based on its anatomical structure.
2
Identify the ruling on teeth
The diyah for each tooth, regardless of type (incisor, canine, molar), is five camels. All teeth are equal in diyah.
This equality is explicitly stated in the hadith.
3
Locate the evidence from hadith
The proof comes from the hadith of Umar ibn Hazm (Nasa'i, Abu Dawood) which states: 'For fingers, ten ten; for teeth, five five; for a mudihah (scalp wound), five.' Also, the hadith of Ibn Abbas (Abu Dawood) states: 'The fingers are equal, and the teeth are equal, the incisor and the molar are equal.'
These are ahad hadith, but they are accepted as sufficient evidence for fiqh rulings.
4
Understand the equality principle
The hadith explicitly declare all fingers equal and all teeth equal. This means no differentiation between index and little finger, or between incisor and molar. The diyah for any finger is ten camels, and for any tooth is five camels.
The equality applies to the diyah amount, not to the number of joints or root structure.

نصف دية الاصبع [ ۲۳۵۰] (۱۴) وفى كل سن خمس من الابل والاسنان والاضراس کلها سواء [۲۳۵۱] (۱۵) ومن ضرب عضوا فاذهب منفعته ففيه دية كاملة كما لو قطعه تین اونٹ اور ایک تہائی اونٹ لازم ہوں گے۔ یا 33.33 دیناریا 333.33 درہم لازم ہوں گے۔ اور جس انگلی میں صرف دوگر ہیں ہیں جیسے انگوٹھے کی انگلی تو ایک گرہ کٹنے سے ایک انگلی کی آدھی دیت لازم ہوگی یعنی پانچ اونٹ۔ یا۵۰ دینار یا ۵۰۰ پانچ سو درہم لازم ہوں گے ۔ کیونکہ حساب سے یہی بنتا ہے۔ اثر میں ہے۔ عن عمر بن الخطاب قال فی کل انملة ثلث دية الاصبع وفي حديث عكرمة عم عمر ثلث قلائص و ثلث قلوص (الف) (۲) عن ابراهيم قال في كل مفصل من الاصابع ثلث دية الاصبع الا الابهام فانها مفصلان في كل مفصل النصف (ب) (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب الا صبح ج تاسع، ص ۳۸۵ نمبر ۷۷۰۴/۱۷۷۰۵ ار مصنف ابن ابی هیته ۳۸ کم فی کل اصبح ج خامس ،ص ۱۳۶۹، نمبر ۲۶۹۹۴) اس اثر سے مسئلہ کی وضاحت ہوگئی۔ لف مفاصل : مفصل کی جمع ہے گرہ، جوڑ ۔ (۲۳۵۰) (۱۴) اور ہر دانت میں پانچ اونٹ ہیں۔ اور دانت اور داڑھیں سب برابر ہیں۔ چونکہ داڑھ بھی دانت ہی کی طرح ہے اس لئے جتنی دیت دانت کی ہے اتنی ہی داڑھ کی بھی ہے۔ حضرت عمر بن حزم کی حدیث میں ہے۔ وفی الاصابع عشر عشر وفي الاسنان خمس خمس وفي موضحة خمس (ج) (نسائی شریف، باب ذکر حدیث عمرو بن حزم في المعقول ص ۶۶۹ نمبر ۴۸۶۰ ابوداؤد شریف، باب دیات الاعضاء ص ۲۷۸ نمبر ۴۵۶۳) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ ہر دانت میں پانچ پانچ اونٹ ہیں۔ اور سب دانت برابر ہیں اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن ابن عباس ان رسول الله الله قال الأصابع سواء والاسنان سواء الثنية والضرس سواء هذه و هذه سواء (د) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب دیات الاعضاء ص ۲۷۸ نمبر ۴۵۵۹) اس حدیث سے پتا چلا کہ تمام دانت اور تمام انگلیوں کا درجہ برابر ہے۔
Half diyah of the finger. [2350] (14) And for every tooth, five camels, and all teeth and molars are equal. [2351] (15) And whoever strikes a limb and removes its function, then full diyah is due upon him, just as if he cut it off. Three camels and one-third camel will be due. Or 33.33 dinars or 333.33 dirhams will be due. And for the finger that has only two joints, like the thumb, when one joint is cut, half the diyah of one finger is due, i.e., five camels, or 50 dinars, or 500 five hundred dirhams. Because that is what the calculation yields. There is an athar: from Umar ibn al-Khattab, he said: for every fingertip, one-third of the diyah of the finger. And in the hadith of Ikrimah from Umar: three young she-camels and one-third of a young camel. (a) (2) From Ibrahim: he said: for every joint of the fingers, one-third of the diyah of the finger, except for the thumb, for it has two joints, for each joint half. (b) (Mussannaf Abd al-Razzaq... etc.) This athar clarifies the issue. Explanation: مفاصل is the plural of مفصل, meaning joint. (2350) (14) And for every tooth, five camels. And the teeth and molars are all equal. Since the molar is also like a tooth, so whatever diyah is for a tooth is also for a molar. In the hadith of Umar ibn Hazm: and for fingers, ten ten, and for teeth, five five, and for a mudihah, five (c) (Nasa'i... etc.) From this hadith it is known that every tooth has five camels. And all teeth are equal. Its proof is this hadith: from Ibn Abbas that the Messenger of Allah (saws) said: 'The fingers are equal, and the teeth are equal, the incisor and the molar are equal, this and that are equal.' (d) (Abu Dawood...) From this hadith it is found that all teeth and all fingers have equal status.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hidayah tradition. It states that for cutting off a fingertip (the distal phalanx) of any finger except the thumb, one-third of the diyah of that finger is due. For the thumb, which has only two joints, each joint carries half the diyah of the thumb. The diyah for a finger is ten camels, so a fingertip is 3.33 camels, and a thumb joint is 5 camels. The teeth: each tooth (including incisors and molars) has a diyah of five camels. All teeth are equal in diyah. The proof comes from the hadith of Umar ibn Hazm (recorded by Nasa'i and Abu Dawood) and the hadith of Ibn Abbas (recorded by Abu Dawood) which explicitly state equality of fingers and teeth. The athar from Umar and Ibrahim support the finger joint calculation. The commentary also clarifies that if a limb loses function but remains, full diyah is due, which is a separate but connected ruling meant to be understood in the same context of compensations for injuries.

Key Terms
انملة (anmulah): The tip of a finger, specifically the distal phalanx (the part containing the fingernail).
مفصل (mafsil): A joint of the finger; the articulation between two phalanges.
سن (sinn): A tooth, including incisors, canines, and molars (dars).
ضرس (dirs): A molar tooth.
دية (diyah): Blood money; the financial compensation paid to the victim or his heirs for bodily injury or homicide.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) 10 camels
C) 20 camels
D) 1 camel

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, each tooth (including molars) has a diyah of five camels, and each finger joint (except thumb joints) is one-third of the finger's diyah of ten camels.

Loss of Limb Function and Full Diyah
Ruling: loss of function equals amputation; proof from Umar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that loss of a limb's function warrants full diyah and identify the proof from the practice of Umar ibn al-Khattab.

Core Idea
Loss of function equals loss of limb in Hanafi diyah law.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi principle: when a limb loses its function (e.g., paralysis, blindness) but remains physically intact, the full diyah for that limb is due—just as if it were amputated. We will explore the ruling, its examples, and the proof from the time of Umar ibn al-Khattab, which established this equivalence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the core ruling: full diyah is due for loss of a limb's function, even if the limb remains.
The passage uses the example of a paralyzed hand or blinded eye.
2
Analyze the Examples
Work through the two examples given: a hand that becomes paralyzed and an eye that loses sight. Note that the diyah amount is the same as for amputation.
Diyah for a hand or eye is fifty camels each.
3
Examine the Proof
Review the athar from Umar ibn al-Khattab: a man lost hearing, intellect, tongue, and male organ due to a stone strike; Umar awarded four full diyats while the victim was still alive.
This shows that loss of function of multiple organs results in multiple full diyats.
4
Connect Proof to Principle
Understand how Umar's verdict supports the Hanafi position: each lost function is treated as equivalent to loss of the organ itself for diyah purposes.
The ruling is based on this precedent, showing that function is the essence of the limb's value.
5
Note Differences with Other Schools
Recognize that this ruling is mukhtalaf fih; some other schools may require additional proof or limit this rule to specific limbs.
The Hanafi school relies on Umar's judgment, which is considered a strong precedent (Qawl Sahabi).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۵) کسی نے کسی کے عضو کو مارا جس کی وجہ سے اس کی منفعت چلی گئی تو اس میں پوری دیت ہے۔ جیسے کہ اس کو کاٹ دینے میں ہے۔ جیسے ہاتھ شل ہوگیا اور آنکھ کی روشنی چلی گئی۔ کسی نے کسی کے عضو پر اس طرح مارا کہ عضو تو باقی رہا لیکن اس کا نفع مکمل ختم ہو گیا۔ مثلا ہاتھ پر مارا جس کی وجہ سے ہاتھ تو باقی رہالیکن ہاتھ شل ہو گیا اور کسی کام کا نہیں رہا تو یوں سمجھا جائے گا کہ ہاتھ کٹ گیا۔ اس لئے ہاتھ کی پوری دیت پچاس اونٹ لازم ہوگی۔ یا آنکھ پر مارا جس کی وجہ سے آنکھ تو باقی رہی لیکن اس کی روشنی ختم ہو گئی تو گویا کہ پوری آنکھ ختم ہو گئی۔ اس لئے ایک آنکھ کی پوری دیت پچاس اونٹ لازم ہوگی۔ اثر میں ہے۔ ابا المهلب عم ابي قلابة قال سمعته يقول رمى رجل رجلا بحجر في رأسه في زمان عمر بن الـخـطـاب فـذهـب سمعه وعقله ولسانه وذكره فقضى فيه عمر اربع ديات وهو حى (الف) (سن لليبيتي ، باب اجتماع الضراحات ج نامن ج ۱۷۱، نمبر ۱۶۳۲۶ار مصنف ابن ابی شیبه ۲۴ از اذهب سمعه وبصره ج خامس ، ص ۱۳۵۹، نمبر ۲۶۸۸۳ / مصنف عبد الرزاق، باب من اصیب من اطرافه ما یکون فیه دیتان او ثلاث ج عاشر ص ۱ نمبر ۱۸۱۸۳)
(15) Someone struck a limb of another, causing its function to cease: then full diyah is due, just as if he had cut it off. For example, a hand became paralyzed, or the sight of an eye was lost. If one struck a limb such that the limb remained but its benefit completely disappeared—for example, striking a hand so that the hand remained but became paralyzed and useless—then it is considered as if the hand was cut off. Therefore, the full diyah of a hand (fifty camels) is due. Or if one struck an eye and the eye remained but its light was lost, then it is as if the entire eye was lost, so the full diyah of one eye (fifty camels) is due. There is an athar: Abu al-Muhallab from Abu Qilabah said he heard: A man struck another on the head with a stone during the time of Umar ibn al-Khattab, and he lost his hearing, intellect, tongue, and male organ. So Umar judged four full diyats for him while he was still alive. (a) (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Bab Ijtima' al-Diayat... etc.)
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It states that if a limb is struck so that it loses its function (e.g., paralysis of a hand or blindness of an eye) but the limb remains physically intact, the full diyah for that limb is due, just as if it were cut off. The proof is an athar from the time of Umar ibn al-Khattab: a man was struck on the head and lost hearing, intellect, tongue, and reproductive function; Umar awarded four full diyats (one for each lost function) even though the man was still alive. This demonstrates that loss of function is treated as equivalent to amputation for diyah purposes.

Key Terms
منفعت (manfa'ah): Function or benefit of a limb; its useful capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, if a person's hand is struck and becomes paralyzed (lost function) but remains attached, what diyah is due?
A) Half the diyah of a hand
B) Full diyah of a hand (50 camels)
C) No diyah because the hand is still there
D) One-third of the diyah

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, loss of a limb's function is legally equivalent to amputation for diyah, as demonstrated by Umar's judgment.

Ten Types of Head Wounds (Shijaj) and the Rule of Qisas
Enumeration of head wound types with a qisas rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the ten shijaj in order of severity and state the Hanafi rule on qisas for al-mudihah.

Core Idea
Only al-mudihah (exposing bone) among head wounds allows qisas because it is precisely measurable.

The Hanafi school classifies head wounds into ten categories by depth and severity. This classification is crucial for determining the appropriate penalty: only one type, al-mudihah, qualifies for qisas (retaliation) if inflicted intentionally. The other nine require diyah (blood money) or other compensation. The passage also includes a brief note from the previous lesson about compensation for loss of eye function.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the ten shijaj in order
The list progresses from superficial (al-ḥāriṣah, skin scratch) to severe (al-āmmah, reaching the brain covering). Memorize the sequence: al-ḥāriṣah, al-dāmi‘ah, al-dāmiyah, al-bāḍi‘ah, al-mutalāḥimah, al-simḥāq, al-mūḍiḥah, al-hāshimah, al-munaqqilah, al-āmmah.
The names derive from the wound's physical characteristics.
2
Identify the threshold for qisas
Among the ten, only al-mūḍiḥah (the wound that exposes bone) is eligible for qisas. This is because the bone is clearly visible, allowing precise retaliation without risk of exceeding equivalence.
Deeper wounds like al-hāshimah (fracturing bone) or al-āmmah (reaching the brain) are too risky for precise retaliation.
3
Apply the condition of intentionality
Qisas is only due if the wound was inflicted intentionally (ʿamdan). If accidental, only diyah or compensation applies.
Intent distinguishes criminal liability from civil compensation.
4
Recognize the rule for other wounds
For the remaining nine shijaj, qisas is not applicable. Instead, the perpetrator must pay diyah or other compensation as determined by the depth and consequences.
The specific amounts for each type are detailed in later lessons.
5
Note the appended example about eye diyah
The passage includes a note that if an eye remains intact but loses function, it is treated like complete loss, requiring full diyah (50 camels). This is a continuation from the previous lesson on eye injuries.
This shows how diyah principles extend beyond head wounds.

(۲۳۵۲) (۱۲) والشجاج عشرة الحارصة والدامعة والدامية والباضعة والمتلاحمة والسمحاق والموضحة والهاشمة والمنقلة والآمة [۲۳۵۳] ۱۷ ففى الموضحة القصاص ان كانت عمدا ولا قصاص في بقية جس کی وجہ سے آنکھ تو باقی رہی لیکن اس کی روشنی ختم ہو گئی تو گویا کہ پوری آنکھ ختم ہو گئی۔ اس لئے ایک آنکھ کی پوری دیت پچاس اونٹ لازم ہوگی۔ (  الف  )
(2352) (12) And the head wounds are ten: al-harisah, al-dami'ah, al-damiyah, al-badi'ah, al-mutalahimah, al-simhaq, al-mudihah, al-hashimah, al-munaqqilah, and al-ammah. [2353] 17 For al-mudihah, qisas is due if it was intentional, and no qisas for the rest. Because of which the eye remained but its light went, so it is as if the entire eye was lost. Therefore, the full diyah of one eye, fifty camels, is due. (a)
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement enumerating the ten categories of head wounds (shijaj) and specifying that only among these, al-mudihah (the wound that exposes the bone) allows for qisas (retaliation) if it was intentional. The other nine types do not have qisas; instead, they entail diyah or other compensation. The list starts with the most superficial (al-harisah: skin scratch) and goes deeper to al-ammah (wound reaching the brain covering). The commentary adds that loss of function of an eye also results in full diyah, which is actually a note from the previous lesson repeated here.

Key Terms
شجاج (shijaj): Plural of shajjah; wounds specifically on the head or face that reach the bone.
الموضحة (al-mudihah): A wound that exposes the bone; the fifth shajjah in the list.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Only al-mudihah among the ten shijaj qualifies for qisas if intentional, because its precise measurability allows exact retaliation.

The Ten Categories of Head Wounds (Shijaj) in Hanafi Diyat Law
Defines the ten categories of head wounds in diyat law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the ten types of shijaj in order of severity and define each based on its anatomical depth, and identify which type allows qisas.

Core Idea
Head wounds are classified into ten precise categories for diyat, each with a distinct ruling; only the mawdihah permits qisas.

In Hanafi fiqh, injuries to the head and face are specially categorized as shijaj (plural of shajjah) to determine blood money (diyat) and, in one case, retribution (qisas). These ten categories progress in severity from a simple scrape to a wound penetrating the cranial membrane. Understanding these definitions is essential for correctly applying diyat law.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish shijaj from jirahat
Recognize that shijaj refers exclusively to wounds on the head and face, while jirahat are wounds elsewhere on the body. This is a foundational distinction for diyat calculations.
2
List the ten types in order
Memorize the sequence from least to most severe: al-harisah, ad-dami'ah, ad-damiyah, al-badi'ah, al-mutalahimah, as-simhaq, al-mawdihah, al-hashimah, al-munaqqilah, al-ammah.
The list is given in the source text in that order.
3
Define each by etymology and anatomy
For each term, break down the Arabic root and describe how deep the wound goes. For example, mawdihah comes from wadih (clear) and means the bone is exposed.
The commentary provides Urdu explanations; use the translation for English definitions.
4
Identify the legal consequence of mawdihah
The source states that qisas applies only if the wound is a mawdihah and it was intentional. No other shajjah carries qisas; they are punished with diyat.
This is a key exception among the ten.
5
Apply the definitions to a hypothetical case
Given a description of a head wound, determine its category and whether qisas could be applicable.
This tests understanding of the definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشجاج عشرة الحارصة والدامعة والدامية والباضعة والمتلاحمة والسمحاق والموضحة والهاشمة والمنقلة والآمة [۲۳۵۳] ۱۷ ففى الموضحة القصاص ان كانت عمدا ولا قصاص في بقية (الشرح الثميرى الجزء الثالث) ۳۲۲ كتاب الديات. كاليد اذا شلت والعين اذا ذهب ضوءها ۲۳۵۲ (۱۲) والشجاج عشرة الحارصة والدامعة والدامية والباضعة والمتلاحمة والسمحاق والموضحة والهاشمة والمنقلة والآمة [۲۳۵۳] ۱۷ ففى الموضحة القصاص ان كانت عمدا ولا قصاص في بقية التشريح ان زخموں کی تشریح اس طرح ہے۔ جو زخم چہرہ اور سر پر ہو اس کو مجہ کہتے ہیں اور جو باقی بدن پر ہو اس کو جراحتہ کہتے ہیں (۱) حارصہ جس میں کھال چھل جائے جس کو اردو میں کھرونچ کہتے ہیں (۲) دامعہ : دمع سے مشتق ہے آنسو، جس زخم میں آنسو کے مانند خون ظاہر ہو جائے مگر بہے نہیں (۳) دامیہ : دم سے مشتق ہے، جس زخم سے خون بہہ جائے (۴) با ضعہ : بضع سے مشتق ہے چیرنا، کا شنا، جس زخم میں کھال کٹ جائے (۵) متلاحمہ : لحم سے مشتق ہے گوشت، جس میں گوشت کٹ جائے (۶) سمحاق : سر کی ہڈی اور سر کے گوشت کے درمیان باریک جھلی ہوتی ہے اس کو سمحاق کہتے ہیں، وہ زخم جو اس جھلی تک پہنچ جائے (۷) موضحہ : وضح سے مشتق ہے واضح ہوتا، وہ زخم جس میں ہڈی کھل جائے (۸) ہاشمہ : ہشم کا ترجمہ ہے چورا چورا کرنا۔ یہاں مراد ہے وہ زخم جو ہڈی توڑ دے(۹) منقلہ : نقل سے مشتق ہے، جو ہڈی کو اس کی جگہ سے سرکا دے (۱۰) آمہ : آمہ کا ترجمہ ہے دماغ یا ہڈی کے اندر وہ پردہ جس کے اندر دماغ ہوتا ہے، وہ زخم جو اس پر دے تک پہنچ جائے جس کے اندر دماغ ہوتا ہے۔
Head wounds are ten: al-harisah, ad-dami'ah, ad-damiyah, al-badi'ah, al-mutalahimah, as-simhaq, al-mawdihah, al-hashimah, al-munaqqilah, and al-ammah. The commentary explains: wounds on the face and head are called shijaj; elsewhere, jirahat. (1) Harisah: skin is scraped. (2) Dami'ah: from dam', tears; blood appears like tears but does not flow. (3) Damiyah: from dam, blood; blood flows. (4) Badi'ah: from bad', cut; skin is cut. (5) Mutalahimah: from lahm, flesh; flesh is cut. (6) Simhaq: thin membrane between skull and scalp; wound reaches that membrane. (7) Mawdihah: from wadih, clear; bone is exposed. (8) Hashimah: from hashm, crush; bone is broken. (9) Munaqqilah: from naql, move; bone is displaced. (10) Ammah: brain or the membrane enclosing it; wound reaches that membrane.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage defines the ten types of head wounds (shijaj) that are foundational to the law of blood money (diyat) for injuries to the head and face. The author lists them from the least severe to the most severe. The commentary provides detailed definitions in Urdu. Note that these categories are specific to the head; similar wounds elsewhere on the body are called jirahat. These definitions are crucial because each category has a different prescribed diyat or ruling regarding qisas.

Key Terms
شجاج (shijāj): Plural of shajjah; wounds specifically on the head and face, as opposed to jirahat on other body parts.
موضحة (mawḍiḥah): A wound that exposes the bone; the seventh type of shijaj.
حارصة (ḥāriṣah): A wound where the skin is scraped.
دامعة (dāmiʿah): A wound from which blood appears but does not flow.
دامية (dāmiyah): A wound from which blood flows.
باضعة (bāḍiʿah): A wound that cuts the skin.
متلاحمة (mutalāḥimah): A wound that cuts into the flesh.
سمحاق (simḥāq): A wound that reaches the thin membrane covering the bone (periosteum).
هاشمة (hāshimah): A wound that breaks the bone.
منقلة (munaqqilah): A wound that displaces a bone fragment.
آمة (āmmah): A wound that reaches the membrane enclosing the brain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Al-Mawdihah

D) Al-Munaqqilah

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh divides head wounds into ten categories from the superficial harisah to the brain-penetrating ammah; only the mawdihah allows qisas if intentional.

Qisas for Mawdihah – Limited to Exact Equivalence
States rule of qisas for intentional mawdihah and denies qisas for other head wounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas for head wounds, identify the only wound type eligible for qisas, and explain the rationale of exact equivalence.

Core Idea
Qisas for head wounds is only for mawdihah because exact retaliation must be possible.

This lesson addresses a specific ruling in jinayat: which head wounds permit qisas? The Hanafi school strictly limits qisas to the mawdihah – the wound that exposes bone. For all other shijaj, the remedy is diyat or ta'zir. We will explore the reasoning behind this limitation and the proof from the practice of Sayyiduna Umar (ra).

Guided Path
1
Identify the wound categories
Recognize that head wounds (shijaj) are classified into types: mawdihah (exposing bone) and others (e.g., harisah, dami'ah, etc.).
The text explicitly contrasts mawdihah with the rest (baqiyat al-shijaj).
2
State the ruling for each category
For intentional mawdihah, qisas is established. For all other intentional head wounds, qisas is not permitted; instead, diyat or ta'zir applies.
The ruling is categorical: 'fihi al-qisas' vs 'la qisas'.
3
Understand the rationale of exact equivalence
Qisas requires precise equivalence (musawah). Mawdihah can be matched exactly because the wound is measurable and reproducible. Other wounds vary too much in depth and effect.
The commentary explains: 'al-mawdihah yumkin an yuqasa biha sawa'an bi sawa'in'.
4
Connect to the proof from Umar (ra)
The footnote cites an athar where Umar (ra) awarded four complete diyats for loss of hearing, reason, speech, and memory from a head wound. This illustrates that when precise retaliation is impossible, full diyat is given, supporting the limitation of qisas.
The athar shows that if function is lost, diyat compensates, reinforcing the principle that qisas is only for wounds where exact equivalence is feasible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ففى الموضحة القصاص ان كانت عمدا ولا قصاص في بقية الشجاج [۲۳۵۳] ۱۷ موضحہ میں قصاص ہے اگر جان بوجھ کر زخم کیا ہو اور باقی زخموں میں قصاص نہیں ہے ۔ موضحہ ایسا زخم ہے کہ اس کا قصاص برابر سرابر ہو سکتا ہے اس لئے اگر جان بوجھ کر موضحہ زخم کیا تو قصاص لے سکتا ہے۔ اور باقی زخموں میں برابر سرابر قصاص نہیں لے سکتا اس لئے اس میں قصاص نہیں ہے بلکہ حاکم کا فیصلہ ہے یا دیت ہے۔ حاشیہ : (الف) ابا المهلب عم ابي قلابة قال سمعته يقول رمى رجل رجلا بحجر في رأسه في زمان عمر بن الـخـطـاب فـذهـب سمعه وعقله ولسانه وذكره فقضى فيه عمر اربع ديات وهو حى (الف) (سن لليبيتي ، باب اجتماع الضراحات ج نامن ج ۱۷۱، نمبر ۱۶۳۲۶ار مصنف ابن ابی شیبه ۲۴ از اذهب سمعه وبصره ج خامس ، ص ۱۳۵۹، نمبر ۲۶۸۸۳ / مصنف عبد الرزاق، باب من اصیب من اطرافه ما یکون فیه دیتان او ثلاث ج عاشر ص ۱ نمبر ۱۸۱۸۳) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ عضو باقی رہے اور اس کی منفعت ختم ہو جائے تو اس کی پوری دیت دینی ہوگی کیونکہ وہ عضو بیکار ہوگیا۔
For the mawdihah, there is qisas if it was intentional, and no qisas for the rest of the head wounds. The commentary: Mawdihah is such a wound that its qisas can be exacted exactly; therefore, if one intentionally inflicted a mawdihah wound, the victim may take qisas. For the other wounds, exact retaliation is not possible, so there is no qisas; rather, the judge decides or blood money is paid. Footnote: Abu al-Muhallab narrated: A man struck another on the head with a stone during the time of Umar ibn al-Khattab, and the victim lost his hearing, reason, speech, and memory. Umar adjudicated four complete blood moneys while he was still alive.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This lesson gives a clear ruling: among head wounds, only the mawdihah (exposing bone) is eligible for qisas when done intentionally. The reasoning is precision—qisas requires exact equivalence, and mawdihah can be matched exactly, while other head wounds are not reliably measurable. The footnote illustrates a related principle: if a body part loses its function completely, full diyat is owed.

Key Terms
قصاص (qiṣāṣ): Retaliation in kind for intentional injury.
موضحة (mawḍiḥa): A head wound that exposes the bone.
شجة (shajja): A head wound; plural shijaj.
دية (diya): Blood money paid as compensation for injury or death.

Ḥukūmat al-ʿAdl for Wounds Below Mawḍiḥah
States ruling with evidence for wounds below mawḍiḥah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that head wounds less severe than mawḍiḥah have no fixed diyat and are subject to discretionary assessment by a just arbiter (ḥukūmat ʿadl).

Core Idea
For wounds below mawḍiḥah, no fixed diyat exists—compensation is determined by a just arbiter's assessment.

In Hanafi fiqh, diyat (blood money) is fixed for certain injuries. But what about head wounds that are not as severe as mawḍiḥah (where the bone is exposed)? This lesson clarifies that for the first six categories of head wounds (from ḥāriṣah to simḥāq), no specific amount is prescribed in the primary texts. Instead, a just arbiter (ḥukūmat ʿadl) assesses compensation based on the severity and circumstances, guided by the athar of the Companions and early scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the Arabic phrase 'وفى ما دون الموضحة ففيه حكومة عدل'. This states the core rule: for wounds less than mawḍiḥah, the compensation is determined by a just arbiter's judgment.
This is a general principle in the Hanafi school.
2
Understand the wound categories
The passage lists the six wound types below mawḍiḥah: ḥāriṣah, dāmiʿah, dāmiyah, bāḍiʿah, mutalāḥimah, simḥāq. Know them in order of increasing severity.
Mawḍiḥah is the seventh type (bone exposed) and has a fixed diyat of five camels.
3
Analyze the evidence
The passage cites an athar from Ibrāhīm al-Nakhaʿī: 'For what is less than mawḍiḥah, a judgment.' Also, an athar from Zayd bin Thābit provides a recommended scale: for dāmiyah, one camel; bāḍiʿah, two; mutalāḥimah, three; simḥāq, four; mawḍiḥah, five. This scale is not binding but often followed.
The Hanafi position is based on the absence of explicit nass for these wounds.
4
Distinguish from fixed diyat
Recognize that mawḍiḥah and more severe wounds (e.g., hāshimah, munqilah) have fixed diyat amounts. The six below mawḍiḥah do not; hence the need for ḥukūmat ʿadl.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the scope of fixed vs. discretionary compensation.
5
Note cross-madhhab variation
Other schools (e.g., Shāfiʿī) may prescribe fixed amounts for some of these wounds based on different hadith or qiyās. The Ḥanafī school adheres to the principle of no fixed diyat without clear textual evidence.
This ikhtilāf reflects different methodologies in dealing with gaps in the primary sources.

Source Text

وفى ما دون الموضحة ففيه حكومة عدل [۲۳۵۴] (۱۸) اور موضحہ سے کم زخم میں عادل آدمی کا فیصلہ ہے۔ ا موضحہ زخم سے جو ز خم کم ہے اس میں کوئی متعین دیت حدیث میں نہیں ہے بلکہ جو فیصلہ کر دے انتقالا زم ہوگا۔ البتہ موضحہ میں پانچ اونٹ دیت ہے۔ موضحہ سے پہلے یہ زخم ہیں (۱) حارصہ (۲) دامعہ (۳) دامیه (۴) باضعه (۵) متلاحمه (۶) سمحاق ۔ ان چھ زخموں میں حاکم کا فیصلہ ہے۔ حدیث میں موضحہ کی دیت کا تذکرہ ہے اور اس سے بڑے زخموں کی دیت کا تذکرہ ہے۔ موضحہ سے کم والے زخموں کی دیت کا تذکرہ نہیں ہے اس لئے اس میں حاکم کے فیصلے کے مطابق رقم لازم ہوگی (۲) اثر میں ہے۔ عن ابراهيم قال فيما دون الموضحة حكومة(ب) مصنف ابن ابی هیتا افیما دون الموضحه ج خامس، ص ۳۵۲ نمبر ۷ ۲۶۸۰) اثر میں یہ بھی ہے۔ عن زيد بن ثابت قال في الدامية بعير وفي الباضعة بعيران وفي المتلاحمة ثلاث وفي السمحاق اربع وفى الموضحة خمس (ج) (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب الملطاة وما دون الموضحه ج تاسع ص ۳۱۲ نمبر ۱۷۳۴۲)
For what is less than the mawdihah, there is the judgment of a just person. The commentary: Wounds below mawdihah have no fixed diyat in hadith; rather whatever the judge decides will be binding. However, the mawdihah has five camels. The wounds before mawdihah are: harisah, dami'ah, damiyah, badi'ah, mutalahimah, simhaq. For these six, the judge decides. There is an athar from Ibrahim: 'For what is less than mawdihah, a judgment.' Also an athar from Zayd bin Thabit: 'For damiyah, one camel; for badi'ah, two; for mutalahimah, three; for simhaq, four; for mawdihah, five.'
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This ruling clarifies that head wounds less severe than mawdihah (the first six categories) do not have a fixed blood money prescribed in the primary texts. Therefore, the judge (or a just arbiter) assesses compensation based on the severity and circumstances, applying his discretion. The athar from Zayd bin Thabit, however, provides a recommended scale that judges often follow, though it is not legally binding. This Hanafi position emphasizes the absence of explicit nass for these lesser wounds.

Key Terms
حكومة عدل (ḥukūmat ʿadl): Discretionary compensation assessed by a just arbiter for wounds with no fixed diyat.
موضحة (mawḍiḥah): Head wound that exposes the bone; has a fixed diyat of five camels.
شجاج (shijāj): Head wounds; categorized into ten types, with mawḍiḥah as the seventh.

Accidental Mawdihah: Half of One-Tenth of Diya
States the diyat for accidental mawdihah wound: half of one-tenth of full diya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the diyat amount for an accidental mawdihah wound and explain its derivation from the hadith.

Core Idea
For accidental mawdihah, the fixed diyat is 5 camels, half of one-tenth of full diya.

This lesson focuses on a specific type of wound, the mawdihah, where the bone is exposed but not fractured. The Hanafi school, relying on a direct prophetic statement, prescribes a precise amount of blood money when such a wound is inflicted accidentally. Understanding this ruling demonstrates how a clear hadith text definitively sets a fixed compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is accidental mawdihah wound. The ruling: wajib payment of diyat, specifically half of one-tenth of the full diya.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is a hadith from Abdullah ibn Umar: the Prophet (ﷺ) said, 'For mawadhih, five.' This is an ahad hadith, but its meaning is clear (qat'i al-dalala).
Reference: Sunan Abi Dawud, Kitab al-Diyat, hadith no. 4566.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit word 'khams' (five) directly sets the amount. The word 'mawadhih' is the plural of mawdihah. Thus, for each mawdihah wound, the diyat is five camels.
The full diya is 100 camels; one-tenth is 10, half is 5.
4
Note any ikhtilaf
This ruling is mutually agreed upon (muttafaq alayh) among the schools. There is no significant dispute on this fixed amount.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi school relies on this hadith as the primary dalil, and the ruling is mufta bihi. The authority is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
Tarjih authority: Hadith of Abdullah ibn Umar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفى الموضحة ان كانت خطأ نصف عشر الدية [۲۳۵۵] (۱۹) موضحہ اگر غلطی سے ہوا ہو تو دیت کے دسویں حصے کا آدھا ہے۔ ان او پر گزر چکا کہ موضحہ زخم جان بوجھ کرے تو قصاص لازم ہے۔ اور غلطی سے کرے تو پوری دیت سو اونٹ کا دسواں حصہ یعنی دس اونٹ اور اس دسواں حصے کا بھی آدھا یعنی پانچ اونٹ لازم ہوں گے ۔ یا پچاس دینار یا پانچ سو درہم لازم ہوں گے۔ حدیث میں ہے۔ عن عبد الله بن عمر ان رسول الله الا الله قال في المواضح خمس ( د ) ( ابوداؤد شریف، باب دیات الاعضاء ص ۲۷۸ نمبر ۴۵۶۶ رنسائی شریف، ذکر حدیث عمرو بن حزم في العقول ص ۶۶۹ نمبر ۴۸۶۰) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ موضحہ زخم میں پانچ اونٹ دیت لازم ہوگی ۔
And for the mawdihah, if it was accidental, half of one-tenth of the diya. Commentary: It has passed that for intentional mawdihah qisas is obligatory. For accidental, one-tenth of the full diya of one hundred camels, i.e., ten camels, and half of that one-tenth, i.e., five camels, are obligatory. Or fifty dinars or five hundred dirhams. Hadith: Abdullah ibn Umar narrated that the Messenger of Allah said: 'For mawadhih (plural of mawdihah), five.'
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This lesson specifies the blood money for an accidental mawdihah: half of one-tenth of the full diya (which is 100 camels), thus 5 camels, or their equivalent in gold/silver. The hadith from Abdullah ibn Umar directly states the amount of five, confirming the ruling. The commentary explains the math: one-tenth of 100 is 10, half of that is 5. This fixed amount applies regardless of the victim's gender or status, as per the Hanafi position based on this hadith.

Key Terms
دية (diya): Blood money; full diya is 100 camels or equivalent for a free man.
موضحة (mawdihah): A wound on the head that exposes the bone but does not fracture it.

The Diyat for Hashimah Wound: One-Tenth of Full Diya
States the diyat for hashimah wound with proof from Zayd bin Thabit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the diyat for a hashimah wound and identify the proof from Zayd bin Thabit's athar.

Core Idea
The hashimah wound incurs a fixed diyat of ten camels, one-tenth of the full diya.

Hanafi fiqh precisely categorizes head wounds (shijaj) and assigns fixed diyat amounts. Among them, the hashimah—a wound that breaks the bone—carries a specific compensation of one-tenth of the full diya, i.e., ten camels. This ruling is supported by the athar of the Companion Zayd bin Thabit, who enumerated a systematic scale for head wounds. Understanding this amount is essential for accurate diyat adjudication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wound and its ruling
The passage states: 'And for the hashimah, one-tenth of the diya.' The commentary explains this is 10 camels. So the diyat for hashimah is 10 camels.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the athar of Zayd bin Thabit: 'For mawdihah, five; for hashimah, ten; for munaqqilah, fifteen.' This is a qawl sahabi.
Dalil type: Qawl Sahabi, transmission zanni but meaning clear.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The athar explicitly enumerates the amounts for each wound, making the diyat for hashimah directly clear. There is no ambiguity in the Arabic expression.
The explicit listing makes the dalala qat'iyya (certain in meaning).
4
Consider the broader scale
The footnote extends the scale: from damiyah (1 camel) up to munaqqilah (15). This shows the Hanafi system of graded diyat for head injuries.
This is an expansion probe, but the core ruling is for hashimah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الهاشمة عشر الدية [۲۳۵۶] (۲۰) اور ہاشمہ زخم میں دیت کا دسواں حصہ ہے۔ پوری دیت کا دسواں حصہ دس اونٹ ہوتے ہیں اس لئے ہاشمہ زخم میں دس اونٹ لازم ہوں گے۔ اثر میں ہے . عن زيد بن ثابت انه قال في الموضحة خمس وفي الهاشمة عشر وفي المنقلة خمس عشرة وفي حاشیہ : (الف) حضرت علیؓ نے فرمایا جا ئفہ، مامومہ اور منقلہ زخموں میں قصاص نہیں ہے دیت ہے ( ب ) حضرت ابراہیم نے فرمایا موضحہ زخم سے کم میں عادل آدمی جو فیصلہ کرے اتنی رقم ہے (ج) زید بن ثابت نے فرمایا دامیہ زخم میں ایک اونٹ ہے اور باضعہ میں دو اونٹ ہیں اور متلاحمہ میں تین اونٹ ہیں اور سمحاق میں چار اونٹ ہیں اور موضحہ میں پانچ اونٹ ہیں۔

There is an athar from Zayd bin Thabit: 'For mawdihah, five; for hashimah, ten; for munaqqilah, fifteen.' Footnotes: (a) Ali said: No qisas for ja'ifah, mamumah, and munaqqilah; only diyah. (b) Ibrahim said: Below mawdihah, it is a judgment. (c) Zayd bin Thabit said: For damiyah, one; for badi'ah, two; for mutalahimah, three; for simhaq, four; for mawdihah, five.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This lesson gives the diyat for hashimah (a wound that breaks the bone): one-tenth of the full diya, i.e., 10 camels. This is supported by the athar of Zayd bin Thabit. The footnote also clarifies that for even more severe wounds like munaqqilah (displacement), the diyat is 15 camels, and that qisas does not apply to ja'ifah (penetrating) and mamumah (reaching brain) wounds. This systematic scale shows the Hanafi precision in categorizing diyat amounts for head injuries.

Key Terms
هاشمة (hāshimah): A head wound that breaks the bone (cranium).
موضحة (mawḍiḥah): A head wound that exposes the bone but does not break it.
منقلة (munqilāh): A head wound that displaces the bone.

Discretionary Assessment for Wounds Below Mawdihah
Ruling on wounds below mawdihah requiring judicial assessment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that wounds below mawdihah require a discretionary assessment (hukumat) and distinguish it from fixed diyah for mawdihah and above.

Core Idea
Wounds below mawdihah have no fixed blood money; the judge determines compensation based on severity.

In the Hanafi school, not all head wounds carry a fixed blood money. Only wounds that expose the bone (mawdihah) and above have specified amounts. For lesser wounds, the law grants the judge discretion to award just compensation. This lesson examines the proof and application of this principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wound categories
List the six types of head wounds below mawdihah: harisah, dami'ah, damiyah, badi'ah, mutalahimah, simhaq. Recognize that mawdihah is the threshold for fixed diyah.
These are classifications from shijaj (head wounds).
2
Understand the threshold (mawdihah)
Mawdihah is a wound that exposes the bone. The Hanafi ruling stipulates that only mawdihah and above have fixed blood money (e.g., 5 camels for mawdihah).
Wounds below this are not covered by primary texts.
3
Examine the dalil
The passage cites two athar: one from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i affirming discretionary assessment, and another from Zaid bin Thabit assigning specific camels for some below-mawdihah wounds. The Hanafi school considers the latter as zanni (non-binding).
The Hanafi position is that no fixed amount is established for these wounds.
4
Apply the ruling
For any wound below mawdihah, the judge (hakim adl) makes a discretionary assessment (hukumat al-adl) based on severity, victim's status, and custom.
This ruling is wajib on the judge; the compensation is binding once determined.

Source Text

Book of Blood Money. Head wounds. [2354] (18) And for what is below the mawdihah, there is a discretionary assessment by a just person. [2354] (18) And for a wound less than mawdihah, it is the decision of a just person. For a wound less than mawdihah, there is no fixed blood money in hadith, rather whatever decision he makes will be binding. However, for mawdihah, the blood money is five camels. The wounds before mawdihah are: (1) harisah, (2) dami'ah, (3) damiyah, (4) badi'ah, (5) mutalahimah, (6) simhaq. For these six wounds, the judge's decision applies. In hadith, the blood money for mawdihah is mentioned, and for wounds larger than it; the blood money for wounds less than mawdihah is not mentioned, so the amount becomes binding according to the judge's decision. (2) There is an athar from Ibrahim who said: 'For what is below mawdihah, there is a discretionary assessment.' (b) (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba) And there is also an athar from Zaid bin Thabit who said: 'For damiyah one camel, for badi'ah two camels, for mutalahimah three, for simhaq four, and for mawdihah five.'
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage states the principle for head wounds that are less severe than mawdihah. Mawdihah is a wound that exposes the bone. Wounds less than that—such as those that only scratch the skin (harisah), cause bleeding (dami'ah), cause blood to flow (damiyah), cut the flesh (badi'ah), reach the muscle (mutalahimah), or reach the membrane covering the bone (simhaq)—have no fixed blood money specified in the primary texts. Therefore, the judge (hakim) makes a discretionary assessment (hukumat al-adl) based on the severity, the victim's status, and local custom. The passage also cites an athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i confirming this discretionary assessment, and another from Zaid bin Thabit which assigns specific camel numbers to some of these wounds, indicating that while there is precedent, the definitive ruling is left to the judge. This is a standard Hanafi position: only mawdihah and above have fixed amounts; below that, discretion.

Key Terms
شجاج (shijaj): Head wounds, a category of injuries with specific blood money rulings.
موضحه (mawdihah): A wound that exposes the bone.
حكومة (hukumat): Discretionary assessment by a judge when no fixed amount is prescribed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It has a fixed blood money of five camels
C) The judge makes a discretionary assessment (hukumat)
D) No blood money is due

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, only mawdihah (bone-exposing wound) and above have fixed blood money; all lesser wounds require a judge's discretionary assessment.

Blood Money for Accidental Mawdihah Wound
Fixed diyah for mawdihah when caused by mistake

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the fixed diyah for an accidental mawdihah wound and identify the hadith proof establishing it.

Core Idea
The accidental mawdihah carries a fixed diyah of five camels, as established by the Prophet's explicit statement.

In the previous lesson, we learned that an intentional mawdihah wound requires qisas. But what if the wound was accidental? The Hanafi school relies on a specific hadith to fix the blood money for this case. This passage clarifies the amount and the proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the wound called? What is the ruling when it is accidental? Determine the hukm level (wajib).
The wound is mawdihah, which exposes the bone. Accidental: fixed diyah is wajib.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? Identify the hadith from Abdullah ibn Umar.
The hadith is: 'In mawadih, five (camels).' (Abu Dawud, Nasa'i)
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the exact amount? The explicit number 'five' makes the ruling certain in meaning.
The dalala is qati (definitive in meaning) because of the clear numeral.
4
Note the absence of ikhtilaf
Does any school disagree? The passage indicates this is a settled issue (muttafaq alayh).
The ruling is agreed upon across schools; no dispute is mentioned.

[2355] (19) And for the mawdihah if it is a mistake, half of a tenth of the blood money. [2355] (19) If the mawdihah was caused by mistake, then half of one-tenth of the blood money. It has passed above that if the mawdihah wound is intentional, then qisas is obligatory. And if by mistake, then one-tenth of the full blood money of one hundred camels, i.e., ten camels, and half of that one-tenth, i.e., five camels, become obligatory. Or fifty dinars or five hundred dirhams become obligatory. In a hadith: From Abdullah ibn Umar that the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) said: 'In the mawadih (plural of mawdihah), five (camels).' (Abu Dawud, Sunan, Book of Blood Money of Limbs, p. 278, no. 4566; Nasa'i, Sunan, Mention of the Hadith of Amr bin Hazm on Blood Wits, p. 669, no. 4860). From this hadith it is known that for a mawdihah wound, five camels of blood money are obligatory.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage specifies the diyah for a mawdihah wound when it is accidental (khata'). The full diyah for manslaughter is 100 camels. One-tenth is 10 camels, and half of that is 5 camels. So for an accidental mawdihah, the blood money is 5 camels, 50 dinars, or 500 dirhams. The passage notes that if the mawdihah is intentional, qisas (retaliation) applies. The proof is from a hadith of Ibn Umar where the Prophet (ﷺ) said that for mawadih (plural) the diyah is five (camels). This is a fixed amount in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
موضحه (mawdihah): A wound that exposes the bone; the threshold for fixed diyah.
خطأ (khata'): Accidental or mistaken act, as opposed to intentional ('amd).

Blood Money for Hashimah Wound
Fixed diyah for hashimah which fractures the bone

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the diyah for a hashimah wound and identify the proof from the athar of Zaid ibn Thabit.

Core Idea
The hashimah wound carries a fixed diyah of ten camels, one-tenth of the full blood money.

This passage specifies the blood money (diyah) for a wound that fractures the bone, known as hashimah. The ruling is based on an authoritative athar from the Companion Zaid ibn Thabit, which lists graded amounts for different head wounds. Understanding this helps you see how Hanafi fiqh uses precedent to fix quantifiable penalties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The hashimah wound is a fracture of the bone. Its diyah is ten camels, which is one-tenth of the full blood money.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the athar of Zaid bin Thabit, transmitted in Sunan al-Bayhaqi and Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq.
The athar lists several head wounds with their diyah amounts.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit numerical assignment 'ten' in the athar directly indicates the amount for hashimah, making the dalala (meaning) clear and definitive.
The Arabic word 'عشر' (ten) is unambiguous.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
This ruling is mutually agreed upon (muttafaq alayh) among the schools; there is no significant dispute.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi position
The ruling is established at the level of zahir al-riwayah, making it a standard operative position for fatwa.

The proof is from an athar of Zaid bin Thabit, who listed the diyah amounts for various head wounds in sequence: mawdihah 5, hashimah 10, munaqqilah 15, ma'mumah one-third. This establishes the fixed amount for hashimah in the Hanafi school.
